To Sail The Sea Of Stars

       
I drew in a deep breath and the frigid night air flooded my lungs. By this point, I could barely feel my fingers. I tried to ignore the icy numbness of my hands as I hurried through the dense thicket of trees.
      The wind bit savagely at my face. I didn't even have the moonlight to guide me as I blindly tiptoed with bare feet around the towering trunks. Fortunately, I didn't need my eyes to know the path I had to take. As long as I didn't wander too deep into the forest or too close to the edge of the trees, I would be fine. At least that was what I told myself.
      Even if I returned empty handed, I knew that Cameren would understand. Cameren always understood. Chloe-Jane would understand too. In fact, she would just be happy to see that I had returned unharmed. Clarity would be a different story, though. If I returned empty handed, my older sister would convince Mother that she would be a better fit for the task of retrieving the medicine from the next village over. And as her sister, I couldn't let her be exposed to any more risks.
      I needed to move fast, but not too fast. If I sprinted, I would attract far too much attention and most likely get an arrow in my back-probably multiple arrows. Or I'd be chased until I finally collapsed from sheer exhaustion. But if I was chased, I'd be able to hold my own for a short time. I was a good runner. When I die, I want to be the one to decide where and how.
      If the overseers did notice and pursue me, they would only follow me so far into the trees. They weren't stupid-I had to give them that much-they knew about the bloodthirsty creatures that lurked in the dark. If I had to choose between the overseers and the monsters of the dark, I would gladly put my fate in the hands of the monsters without a second thought. Monster . . . is that really the right term? The title of monster is a much better fit for the overseers than the creatures of the forest.
      No one really knew how many creatures hid in the shadows of the trees, but then again, no one had ever crossed the forest and returned to tell the tale. They either disappeared forever or became the dinner of a fanged fiend.
      Occasionally the bodies of overseers and slaves alike would be found lying at the edge of the forest. Sometimes they were in pieces, other times they were whole. Sometimes their faces were frozen in expressions of horror, other times they looked like they had just gone to sleep after a long day. Or so the rumors claimed. While I had never seen any of these bodies myself, Chloe-Jane had been unfortunate enough to discover the corpse of an overseer once on her way to the field. She wasn't able to sleep for days after that.
      With my breath puffing out in the frigid air in front of me, I continued swiftly on my way. Tipping my head back, I searched the hidden sky, but all I could see was darkness. There wasn't even a sliver of moon tonight, which was perhaps a good thing; the darker it was, the easier it would be for me to go unseen.
      Just as I was about to take another step forward, I suddenly froze at the sound of a twig snapping in the distance. My breath caught in my throat, and I strained my eyes as I squinted through the darkness. A torch flared to life in the distance and the trees glowed with the flickering light of a flame.
      Had the overseers found me?
      I was rooted to the spot as I held my breath. No. They had found someone else.
      I forced myself to move. I put one shaky foot in front of the other as I crept closer, and my heart rate increased with each cautious step. If I was careful, I could make my way past the overseers without being noticed. They were distracted after all. But then what kind of person would that make me?
      As I crept closer, I caught the sound of two gruff male voices.
      "Pretty girl, beautiful girl," a voice crooned, "what are you doing out in the forest by yourself?"
      "Who do you belong to, girl?" the other male voice demanded impatiently.
      "Aw, look at that," the first voice spoke again, cooing in a way that made my skin crawl. "She's too scared to speak. Look, look! She's trembling."
      "If she's the servant of some noble," the second voice said flatly, "we'll be in some serious trouble if we kill her."
      "Are you kidding me? She's obviously trying to run away! What else would she be doing in the forest?"
      "Her clothes look decent enough for working under a minor lord or duke. Say, if we do return her to whoever she ran away from, I bet you we'll be rewarded handsomely."
      "Alright, alright," the first voice said. "But we're not in a rush now, are we?"
      At this point, I was practically standing right behind the overseers. In the fiery light of their torches, I could see the silhouette of a woman outlined against the dark. Her face was hidden by the shadow of her long cloak and her delicate hands hung limply at her sides. The woman couldn't have been a slave like me; her skin was porcelain, almost paler than the moon itself. But her dirt-caked feet were bare just like mine.
      I made my decision in a split second. "Run!" I shouted as I shoved past the shocked overseers. "We need to run!" I seized one of the woman's hands and pulled her along with me.
      "What are you doing?" the woman asked in shock as we sprinted into the trees.
      "I'm saving you, of course. Hurry!"
      I felt the woman's slender hand close around mine as she nodded.
      "Get back here!" one of the overseers yelled angrily. "Stop running!"
      "Both of you are dead now!" the other roared at our backs.
      My heart thundered in my chest as I crashed through the trees. Branches tore at my face and arms as I barreled past with the cloaked woman close at my heels. There was no use trying to keep quiet now. The woman was keeping up with me for now, but how much longer could we run? We might be able to lose the overseers in the trees, but we certainly wouldn't be able to outrun any of the monsters crouching in the dark.
      Suddenly, my tattered skirts snagged on a low-hanging branch and I toppled sideways into a clump of bushes. I bit back my cry as my ankle twisted painfully beneath me. My panic was beginning to take hold of me as I tried and failed to rise to my feet. I struggled against my fear, but I was fighting a losing battle.
      The woman tugged at my hand in an attempt to get me to rise to my feet, but my throbbing ankle refused to support my weight. I can't, I mouthed. I was trying my best to remain calm despite my rapidly spiking heart rate. "Run now while you still can!"
      Suddenly, the glow of torches spilled through the forest, and the woman quickly pushed me behind the clump of shrubbery. After crouching down, she put a finger to her lips in a gesture of silence. The rest of her face was still hidden in the shadows of her hood.
      The voice of the first overseer floated over to us. "I heard something coming from over there."
      "Let's just leave them," the second voice said nervously. "We've gone too deep into the forest. And for all we know, those girls might have already been taken by some monster."
      "Nonsense," the first voice scoffed. "I'm telling you-I'm finding those silly girls. What are you? Afraid?"
      "There!" the second voice said excitedly. Torchlight flooded my vision as the overseer pointed the flame at our hiding place. I made to jump to my feet, but the woman put a light hand on my shoulder, silently instructing me to remain where I was.
      I opened my mouth, but no sound came out. Instead I just watched in a mixture of confusion and horror as the woman rose to face the overseers.
      "Hasn't anyone told you?" The woman's calm voice cut through the silence. "Sometimes being afraid of the dark is a good thing. Sometimes fearing the night can keep you alive."
      "How dare you speak to us that way!" the first overseer roared as his face contorted in rage.
      The second overseer held his torch higher and the woman's pale skin glowed with a fiery sheen in the light. "Know your place, girl. Who do you think you are?"
      "Who do I think I am?" the woman echoed quietly as she angled her head to the side. "Who do you think you are, human?"
      My eyes widened as the woman slowly reached up to remove her hood. Her back was to me, but the expressions on the overseers' faces revealed everything.
      "V-vampire!" the second overseer shrieked. His torch clattered to the forest floor, and my blood went cold as the flames sputtered out.
      Vampire.
      "Run!" the first overseer yelled shrilly. He discarded his torch too and began to sprint back the way they had come.
      The second overseer had dropped to his knees and was now clasping his hands together in prayer. "Have mercy!"
      Suddenly, the woman's voice whispered in my ear, and I nearly jumped out of my skin. "Close your eyes."
      I squeezed my eyes shut. My heart was beating so fast that I was afraid it would give out at any moment. There was a rush of wind, followed by the sound of screaming. I clapped my hands over my ears. I felt light headed. What was going to happen to me?
      The woman's voice returned. "It's over."
      Ever so slowly, I cracked open an eye. I was finally able to see the woman's face, which was illuminated by a patch of moonlight. She appeared no older than myself. She was beautiful, and her delicate features reminded me of a princess I had once seen in a painting years ago. But the most captivating feature was the young woman's eyes. Her blood-red irises seemed to glow as she looked down at me.
      "Are you cold?" she asked softly.
      "I-I'm sorry?"
      "You're shivering."
      I blinked. I hadn't even registered that I was shaking all over.
      "Take this," the vampire said as she unfastened her cloak and dropped it at my feet. Her inky hair cascaded down her back, stopping just shy of her heels.
      I hesitantly picked up the cloak with a shaky hand. "I-thank you."
      "What are you doing in the forest?" the vampire asked quizzically. "Are you running away?"
      "No. I'm trying to get to the next slave village. I'm supposed to meet someone there."
      The vampire's eyes glowed as they seemed to stare into my very soul. "You've made it this far. Are you sure you don't want to escape?"
      I stared back, refusing to back down from her gaze. "My family needs me. I won't let them down."
      "If that's the case, you should continue on your way. I can assure you that you won't encounter anything on your way to or back from the village."
      I nodded my head, still in a state of shock.
      "Then I wish you good luck." There was a gust of wind and I snapped my eyes shut for a second time. When I opened them, the dark-haired young woman had vanished into the night.
      It took me a long moment to register what exactly had just happened. First, I was still alive. Second, the young woman who I had tried to save turned out to be a vampire. And third, the overseers who had pursued us were now dead.
      My heart rate had yet to return to normal. After fastening the vampire's cloak over my shoulders, I climbed to my feet and winced as my ankle barked in protest. Fortunately enough, nothing seemed to be broken. I needed to get out of the forest as fast as I could. The girl with the medicine was waiting for me, and I couldn't afford to keep her out all night.
       
~
       
      By the time I reached the rendezvous site, the temperatures had dropped even lower.
      "Sister," a voice called out. Leaves rustled above me, then the medicine girl dropped to the forest floor. She and I had established that it was safer for her to stay hidden until I arrived.
      "We shouldn't stick around here too long," I said as a way of greeting.
      The girl just nodded as she looked up at me. Her dark skin glowed in the moonlight. She was no older than ten, yet her dark eyes held both the maturity and knowledge of any adult.
      "Here." The girl pressed a small cloth pouch into my hands. "How is your brother's recovery going?"
      "It's a slow process," I told her tiredly. "But thanks to you, at least he hasn't gotten worse."
      The girl nodded her head. "That's good." Then her gaze turned serious. "We both should get back."
      "Thank you," I whispered. "Are you sure there isn't anything I can give you in return?"
      The girl shook her head. "No need, sister. Everyone here is family. Where would we be if we didn't look out for each other? Now, hurry back to your immediate family. They're waiting for you."
       
~
       
      "Corinna!" Chloe-Jane's whisper greeted me as I pushed open the door to our tiny house. "You're back!"
      "And you're still awake." I closed my eyes to relish the warmth from the small fire burning in the hearth. My entire body was frozen, and the shock of the night's encounter still had yet to wear off.
      When I opened my eyes, I found that Chloe-Jane had rushed forward to wrap her arms around me in a tight hug. Even though Chloe-Jane recently turned fourteen, she had both the appearance and spirit of a girl much younger.
      "I tried to get her to go to sleep, but our stubborn sister kept refusing," Clarity said from the back of our one-room house. Her arms were folded over her chest.
      Chloe-Jane pouted as she finally released me. Her thick, wavy hair was pulled back in a messy braid, and I reached out to tuck a flyaway strand behind her ear. I couldn't be bothered to fix my own hair; I settled to simply braid back the curls, and after my encounter in the forest, the number of flyways was a lost cause.
      I glanced over at Mother and Cameren. The two of them were fast asleep on the pile of straw that made up our bed. Even in sleep, they looked completely exhausted.
      I was pretty sure my sisters had noticed my new cloak, but neither of them commented on it. I unfastened the vampire's cloak. After shaking it out, I folded it into a neat square before setting it next to the door. I set the medicine down as well. Next, I limped over to the hearth and took a seat on the dirt in front of the dancing flames. I could tell that Clarity was eyeing my limp, but I turned to Chloe-Jane and patted the ground next to me. "Come here."
      Chloe-Jane hurried over and plopped down at my side.
      "I'll rebraid your hair for you," I told my sister. "Then it's off to bed for all of us."
      "That means you too, Clarity," Chloe-Jane said, grinning. Then she turned so that her back was facing me. I skillfully unbraided Chloe-Jane's hair before carefully combing through the it with my fingers. As far as appearances went, Chloe-Jane looked different from the rest of us. Her skin was a shade of toasted caramel rather than the deep brown the rest of us shared. But that was because she had a different father than Clarity, Cameren, and me.
      "Unlike the two of you," Clarity said as she looked down her nose at us, "I happen to be an adult."
      Chloe-Jane crossed her arms over her chest. "You're just jealous that no one does your hair anymore!"
      Clarity gave the two of us an amused look. "That's because I'm an adult. I know how to do my own hair."
      I just shook my head as I smiled at their exchange.
      Suddenly, Chloe-Jane turned to look up at me. "I prayed for you for hours, and it looks like the Immortal One listened!"
      "Is that so?" I gently turned Chloe-Jane's head forward again to continue her braid. "And what exactly did you pray for?"
      "I prayed that you wouldn't meet any overseers or vampires in the forest!"
      I nearly choked on my laugh.
      In truth, there was no Immortal One. The Immortal One was an image created by one of the former kings of the western kingdom. But for Chloe-Jane, the Immortal One was her best friend. I had gently tried to explain to her numerous times before that He wasn't real. She refused to believe me, though, so eventually I came to terms with letting her be.
      Most of the older slaves in the village knew that the Immortal One was a made-up concept; they had come from a land where they worshiped their own gods. It was the young ones who were easily influenced. All the children who weren't old enough to work in the fields were subjected to indoctrination classes. I still remembered my own time there and how much of a joke I thought the Immortal One sounded like. Chloe-Jane, on the other hand, had fallen head over heels for Him.
      "Thank you," I said. "I appreciate it. Look-I've finished your hair. Off to bed with you."
      "Alright, alright." Chloe-Jane bounced to her feet and hurried over to the straw bed to snuggle up beside Mother. "But can you sing me Mother's lullaby?"
      Clarity let out an exasperated noise. "Mother already sang it for you when you were supposed to have gone to sleep all those hours ago, remember?"
      I brushed off my older sister's words and instead walked over to lie down next to Chloe-Jane. "I'll sing. I'll have to do it quietly, though."
      Chloe-Jane closed her eyes. "I know. Whisper it."
      And so I began to quietly sing the lullaby Mother sang to us every night. It was a lullaby in the language of the people we once were. A lullaby of a land that never knew winter. A lullaby of a home I'd never see.
      The song was the only thing I had of the language of Mother's homeland. Mother, Father, and Cameren were born in that faraway land of the southern continent. When Cameren was no more than a year old, the three of them had been stolen away with two hundred others.
      Mother still told us stories of her home, but it was obvious that she had long ago given up hope of ever returning. Even so, she had tried her best to teach us the language of her people in secret many times before. But it had proven difficult because there was little time for us to practice. And if we were caught, well, sometimes I wasn't sure if the consequences were worth it.
      By the time I finished the song, Chloe-Jane had fallen fast asleep. I closed my eyes as well. My own exhaustion was rapidly catching up to me. But before I could sleep, the face of the forest vampire flickered across my mind. I had been warned time and time again that the vampires were cruel and ruthless creatures-that they were monsters wearing human skin. But I hadn't sensed either of those things from the dark-haired vampire. It was true that she had killed the overseers, but I couldn't blame her.
      Vampires were human once. Maybe some of them had been able to hold onto their humanity and not give in completely to their bloodlust.
       
~
       
      I woke to the sound of horns blaring.
      Wincing as the noise shot knives through my skull, I slowly sat up and rubbed the sleep from my eyes. The windowless room was still dark, despite the small fire burning in the hearth.
      Mother, Cameren, and Clarity were already up, and the latter was shaking Chloe-Jane awake. I groggily put out the fire and followed my siblings out the door as we did every morning. There was still a good hour until sunrise, and the air felt sharp like ice.
      I limped toward the middle of the village to collect my breakfast, and when I finished eating, my sisters and I bid goodbye to Mother and Cameren. Because of Mother's age and Cameren's injuries, the two of them were assigned to different work. I followed the line of slaves trudging toward the fields. As we walked, I winced as the sound of whips cracked in the distance and my body tensed up instinctively. Winter was rapidly approaching, and the question of who would survive another year was hanging heavily over our heads.
      "Girl!" a gruff voice called.
      I froze midstep.
      "Yes, you," the voice said impatiently. There was a loud crack, then something sharp hit the back of my legs. I hissed as I was knocked to my knees. Pain shot through my calves and my hands clenched in the fabric of my skirts. "Get up and face me."
      With my calves stinging, I rose to my feet and slowly turned to face the overseer. I rounded my shoulders and dropped my gaze to the man's dirt-stained boots.
      "Why are you limping?" the overseer demanded.
      I continued to stare at the ground. "Forgive me, sir. I was collecting firewood, and I tripped because of the dark." Lie, lie, lie. These were always dangerous waters.
      The overseer turned, already losing interest. "Don't go around injuring yourself like that. Injured slaves aren't any use to us, you know."
      I bowed my head to the overseer before turning to catch up with Chloe-Jane and Clarity.
      "What was that about?" Chloe-Jane whispered worriedly. "Are you alright?"
      "He wanted to know why I was limping," I explained tiredly.
      "I heard. But you weren't collecting firewood last night . . ."
      "It was dark in the forest. You don't need to worry, though-I really just stumbled over some branches."
      Chloe-Jane chewed her lip but nodded all the same.
      When we arrived at the fields, I crouched down to begin the day's work. It was the same every day: the mornings and afternoons were spent picking a silk-like flower. The evenings were spent weeding.
      Clarity dropped down beside me, and we began to work our way through the dew-covered greenery. "Whose cloak were you wearing last night?" my older sister asked quietly.
      "Cloak?"
      Clarity gave me a sideways look. "Don't act so surprised. Did you really think I wouldn't notice?" Unlike Chloe-Jane, who would believe almost anything she was told, Clarity was naturally suspicious. She had a tendency not to trust anything she was told until she verified it for herself, and she didn't seem to trust anyone but herself.
      "I met someone in the forest." I continued to carefully remove the stringy flowers from their stems. I ignored the way they stung at my hands. I saw no point in lying to my sister, especially when she would see right through me. "A young woman-about my age. She had run into a couple of overseers, and I helped her escape."
      "Overseers?"
      "Don't act so surprised," I said lightly as I echoed Clarity's own words. "This isn't the first time I've gotten away. I'll just make sure to keep my head down for a few days. The last time I nearly got caught was a mistake on my part. And I won't make the same mistake twice."
      Clarity was still giving me the side eye, but she remained silent as she waited for me to go on.
      "I'm going to try to return it the next time I go see the medicine girl."
      "Good," Clarity said shortly. "We don't need an overseer finding it in our house when they do their weekly inspections."
      I finally looked up at my sister. "It's getting colder. Do you think we'll be okay?"
      "We've managed before." Clarity turned back to the flowers and skillfully plucked the blossoms with her callused fingers. "We'll manage again."
      I let out a heavy sigh. I hoped Clarity was right. The alternative wasn't something I wanted to think about.

Chapter Two
       
I sat in the heart of the forest. Last week I sensed the presence of the slave woman in the forest, but I never expected her to run in and save me. Save me. A rare smile flickered across my lips. If anyone was in danger, it was the overseers, but that young woman risked her life for my sake. Maybe she was brave. Or maybe she was a fool.
      I lifted my hand to my face-the hand the young woman had grabbed as she pulled me along-and stared absently at my palm. Immortality was a curse. If I ever found Karmen, the queen of vampires, I would put an end to her to ensure she wouldn't share her curse with anyone else again. But I had given up my search years ago. How was I supposed to find the queen when I didn't have the slightest clue what she looked like? My only memory of Karmen was a pair of blood-red eyes glowing from the shadowy depths of a hooded cloak.
      I can give you the strength you need to take your revenge, Karmen had told me. I can give you the power you desire.
      When Karmen had offered me her blood to transform me into a vampire, I drank it without hesitation. I hadn't been thinking about the future, and it certainly hadn't crossed my mind where I'd be now-alone in a forest. There weren't even any animals to keep me company. It was almost as if they could sense me and fled from my presence. I chewed my lip but stopped when my fangs drew blood. Not even the wind was brave enough to whisper through the heavy foliage.
      It had been years now since I'd laid eyes on another vampire. I didn't mind, though. Vampires were difficult to deal with at times-I could never tell the ones who sided with the queen apart from the ones who opposed her. Most vampires, like myself, spent their days confined to the shadows of the forests around the northern continent or hiding away in the capital city.
      I could confidently say that there were no vampires on the sunny southern continent because too much exposure to direct sunlight causes our skin to crack and flake off. The sun in the southern sky was rumored never to set; it's the last place any vampire would go. Even if a vampire's skin did peel off, it would grow back within a day as long as the vampire stayed out of the sun. Just like how everything regenerated within a matter of days. Skin, hair, limbs-everything grows back as if it was never damaged in the first place. Not even a scar is left behind.
      I rose to my feet. I had sat here long enough, and it was time for me to make my rounds.
      The forest was dark-all except for the faint moonlight that danced through the leaves high above. Suddenly, I sensed a human presence entering the forest, and I quietly made my way toward it. After perching in a tree, I blinked down in surprise as my eyes landed on the same dark-skinned woman I'd seen a week ago.
      "Hello?" the young woman whispered into the dark as she nervously glanced around the trees. "I want to return your cloak."
      I dropped from the branch I had been standing on and landed on the ground behind the young woman. "I'm right here."
      The young woman jumped and whirled around to face me. The scent of her fear practically shoved its way up my nose as her brown eyes met mine. "This," she stammered, "this is for you." She held out my cloak with shaky hands. The thick cloth was folded in a neat square.
      I just looked at her. "You don't want it?"
      The young woman held my gaze despite the fact that she was trembling all over. "It belongs to you. I couldn't take it."
      "What if it's a gift?"
      "A gift?"
      I tilted my head to the side. "You're cold."
      "I am," the young woman admitted. "But if the overseers find this in my family's house during their weekly inspection . . ."
      "Then how about you only wear it in the forest? If you only wear it in the forest, the overseers will never need to know."
      "Are you saying I should leave it in the forest?"
      I nodded.
      "Can-can I really do that?"
      I absently took a step closer. "I don't see why not." The young woman flinched but didn't retreat. I stopped as I watched her eyes. "Are you afraid of me?"
      "I am," the young woman breathed. Well, I appreciated her honesty.
      That deep, hidden sadness within me sparked up again and I dropped my gaze. "You're not the only one. Sometimes I'm afraid of myself as well." There was a long pause before I spoke again. "Wear the cloak to wherever your destination is. You can leave it with me when you return back to your village. I'll lend it to you again the next time you're in the forest."
      I turned to walk away, but the young woman called out to me. "Wait!"
      I paused and looked back over my shoulder.
      "I-my name is Corinna. What's your name?"
      My name? It was a name I hadn't used in decades. A name I had forgotten again.
      "You can call me Talon," I said softly.
      "Okay." Corinna nodded her head and her curls bounced with the movement. "Thank you, Talon. I-I guess I'll be on my way then."
      I watched silently as Corinna unfolded the cloak and draped it over her shoulders. After bowing her head, Corinna turned and hurried off.
      Corinna would have to do something about her bare feet if she didn't want to lose them to the cold. I lifted my head to the leafy canopy as if I could see the sea of stars glittering beyond the foliage. Alone again, I found my mind wandering. I couldn't just sit in this forest forever. But where would I go? Where could I go?
      Unfortunately, death was not the simplest option. The only ways a vampire can really die is if they are decapitated or if their body is burned to ashes. Both methods are said to be painful; vampires can feel the curse within them reeling as it struggles to stay alive. I wasn't desperate enough yet to attempt either of the two.
       
~
       
      A week later, I heard the sound of Corinna's footsteps draw near. "Talon?" she called quietly through the dark.
      I rose to my feet and glided through the trees to meet her.
      "Everything went smoothly with the medicine girl tonight." Corinna unfastened the cloak and held it out to me. "Here."
      I hesitated for a moment before I took the cloak from her and tucked it under my arm. "Are you sure you're going to be alright in the cold?"
      Corinna angled her head to the side. "You almost sound as if you're worried about me."
      "Do I?" A faint smile flickered across my lips. Suddenly, I heard Corinna's heart rate quicken-she had caught a glimpse of my fangs. My smile faded. "You should go."
      Not needing to be told twice, Corinna hurried out of the forest. I watched her retreat until she vanished behind the trees.
      I held the cloak in front of me. It was never mine in the first place. Its original owner was long dead.
      I turned on my heel and made my way through the trees until I reached a large hollowed-out log and knelt down to rummage around inside. My fingers traveled over a couple of cloaks, a handful of daggers and other blades, and then finally-shoes! I triumphantly pulled out a pair of scuffed winter boots and set them down on the ground beside me. They looked slightly larger than Corinna's feet, but they would do.
      It had always been in my nature to help people. Those who showed me kindness would be repaid. Those who showed me malice would also be repaid.
      Then I frowned. Karmen. She constantly plagued my thoughts, but it couldn't have just been me, though-I was confident that she wasn't someone anyone could easily forget. It was true that she saved me, but at the same time, she condemned me to this immortal life.
      I let out a humorless laugh. If I ever did come face to face with Karmen, I would kill her. Or at least I would try my hardest to. If the queen of the vampires had been a good person, that would have been one thing, but Karmen was ruthless. She had gone on killing sprees in the distant past, murdering humans and vampires alike in cold blood. To have been turned personally by such a cruel and heartless person . . . I couldn't help but wonder why she did it.
      Even though I was a vampire now too, I still had morals and Karmen needed to be held accountable for her lack of morality.
       
~
       
      Another week went by until the young woman entered the forest again, and this time I emerged from the trees slowly so as to not startle her. "Here." I dropped the cloak into Corinna's surprised arms. After she hastily fastened the cloak around her shoulders, I placed the winter boots at her feet.
      "Are these for me?" Corinna asked with wide eyes.
      "I don't need them. They'll be more useful to you than me."
      "If you don't mind me asking"-Corinna bent down to shove her feet into the boots-"who exactly did these belong to?"
      "They belonged to a slave man who wandered into the forest many years ago."
      "This man . . . what happened to him?"
      "He died."
      "You killed him."
      "I did."
      "How could you?" Corinna's voice dropped to a whisper. Her hands stilled briefly over the laces of the boots. "He was only seeking freedom."
      "You knew from the start how I acquired these shoes. And yet you still chose to wear them."
      Corinna raised her eyes to meet mine. "I need to do what I can to keep myself alive. Otherwise I won't be able to help my family survive."
      "How old are you, Corinna?"
      "I don't know my exact birthdate, but I'm seventeen summers old."
      Seventeen. She was only a year younger than I was when I was turned into a vampire-when I was trapped as my eighteen-year-old self for all of eternity. "I found the man in the forest," I said quietly. "I gave him a choice the same way I give all of those who wander into my forest a choice. I told him that I could guide him through the forest to freedom. Or if he so desired, I could end his life in a painless way. He chose the second option."
      Corinna's eyes widened. "You've helped people escape to freedom?"
      I nodded. "I've noticed a trend in the people who do try to make their way through the forest: if they're traveling alone, they're more likely to pick death than the people traveling with others."
      "Of course." Corinna stood back up. "If you don't have anyone to live for, then why continue to suffer?"
      "Some live for revenge. Some live with the hope that things will eventually change for the better."
      Corinna crossed her arms over her chest as she looked sideways at me. "And what about you, then?"
      "I just don't want to die yet. I've grown too used to living."
      Corinna let out a laugh. "That's certainly one reason to keep living. Anyway," she continued as her smile faded, "I need to be on my way. Unless you want to walk with me."
      Not having anything better to do, I agreed.
      The two of us walked silently through the trees. I walked in front while Corinna lagged a few paces behind. I didn't blame the young woman for not wanting to put her back to me. If I were in her shoes, I would be just as cautious as she was.
      Corinna finally broke the silence. "I've been wondering for a while, what other kinds of creatures inhabit this forest?"
      "I'm the only one in this forest."
      "Just you?" Corinna said in surprise.
      "Yes."
      Corinna fell silent at that, and neither of us spoke until Corinna reached her destination. I faded back into the shadows of the forest and stayed hidden until she finished talking to the young girl who had leaped down from the trees.
      When Corinna started on her way back, I joined her once we were out of sight. "What happened to your brother?" I asked, curious about the snippets of conversation I had overheard during their swift exchange.
      "He was sent off to fight in the war several months back when the king ordered all able-bodied men eighteen years old and up to serve," Corinna explained sadly. "Even though they all went through intensive training, my brother returned a few months later with horrible injuries. In fact, they were so severe that the knights sent him home after they deemed that he was too incapacitated to be of any use. We're trying to do everything we can to help him recover, but he still hasn't gotten much better. The medicine I get here is the only thing keeping him from getting worse."
      "Wasn't there a similar draft ten years ago?" I furrowed my brow as I tried to remember.
      Corinna nodded grimly. "That time, my father had no choice but to go fight. After all these years, the war still rages on and my father hasn't returned home."
      "Have you ever considered the possibility that your father is-"
      "Dead?" Corinna sighed heavily. "Yes. Yes, I have. That's very likely too. But false hope is better than no hope at all. Hope is what keeps all of us going." When we reached the border of the forest and her village, Corinna removed the cloak and boots before handing them back to me. "Thank you, Talon," she said quietly. "Until next time, then."
      I inclined my head as I watched Corinna turn and stealthily hurry back into the village. When she vanished from my line of sight, I turned and made my way back into the heart of the forest. I had to admit that her company was a nice change.
      Suddenly, I stilled. Someone had just entered the forest and was moving rapidly through the trees. This wasn't a human presence.
      Narrowing my eyes, I leaped into the branches high above and scanned the surrounding forest for signs of movement. Whatever had entered the forest was moving fast-too fast.
      It was a vampire.
      Just as I was debating whether to stay put or chase this other vampire out myself, the presence vanished. I dropped silently to the floor and made my way to the place where I had sensed the disappearance.
      The sun had slowly begun to rise and I hissed quietly as a handful of blinding rays found their way through the gaps in the canopy of leaves high above. Dew sparkled among the fallen leaves and grass as I made my way toward the edge of the forest.
      I halted. My suspicions were confirmed as my gaze traveled over the forest floor. Deep crimson blood pooled around the severed head of a long-haired male, whose body lay a short distance away.
      I wrinkled my nose as I knelt down to examine the corpse. I could tell that a dagger had been used to sever the head from the body, but the vampire's killer had long vanished. Whoever had sliced the head from the body was not human-the precision of the cut was a dead giveaway that the killer possessed an immense amount of inhuman strength. Whoever they were, they knew how to wield a blade.
      I gingerly lifted the vampire's head from the ground. My eyes widened as I stared at the lifeless face. I had met this vampire once many years ago. I couldn't recall his name, but I vaguely remembered his face. About fifty years ago, I attended a meeting of vampires regarding what to do about Karmen. Half of the vampires didn't think that Karmen deserved to hold the title of queen, while the other half tried to convince them to all pledge allegiance to her. This vampire sat a few seats away from me at the meeting.
      This vampire was fleeing, I realized. That was why he was moving so quickly. He was trying to run away from whoever beheaded him. Then a frown crept onto my face. All the vampires in the area knew this was my forest. Had he purposely been trying to lead his pursuer here? Had he been hoping that they would find and kill me instead?
      After setting the head back down on the ground, I moved over to the body to see if there were any other clues leading to the killer's identity. My eyes traveled to the vampire's outstretched hands, and I blinked. Once. Twice. There, etched into the dirt, were letters. I tilted my head sideways as I tried to make out the crudely drawn shapes:
      K A R M
      The vampire hadn't finished spelling out the name, but I didn't need the remaining letters to figure out who the culprit was.

    
Chapter Three
       
"Corinna!" I awoke to Chloe-Jane shaking my shoulders.
      I blinked groggily. "Is it that time already? I don't hear the wake-up horns."
      Chloe-Jane shook her head. "No, we still have a few minutes. But look! It's snowing!"
      Suddenly wide awake, I jumped to my feet and hurried over to the door. After opening it just enough to press my eye to the opening, I peeked outside. Glittering flakes drifted down from the overcast sky and covered the ground in white powder. The first snow of the year.
      "It's beautiful," Chloe-Jane said dreamily from behind me. "Isn't it? Isn't it?"
      Clarity yawned from our straw bed before I could respond. "It's cold. Shut the door, will you?"
      "Fine, fine," Chloe-Jane huffed. She irritably crossed her arms over her chest as I carefully latched the door.
      "It's freezing outside," I observed. "Is it too much to hope that they'll give us proper shoes this year?"
      Cameren sat up to join our conversation. "The likelihood that we'll be given shoes is not very high. The king doesn't care about us-the overseers don't care about us either."
      "You almost make it sound as if that's a bad thing," Chloe-Jane said as she pursed her lips.
      "She's right." Clarity yawned again. "Once you get noticed, there's no guarantee that you'll make it out in one piece."
      I nodded along. Since our farm was located near the edge of the inner kingdom, we tended to be forgotten when it came to the distribution of clothing and food. At least we were still part of the inner kingdom. It was hard to think about those even farther away from the capital.
      Fortunately for us, we had come up with a temporary solution. I hurried over to the corner of our small house to retrieve the straw shoes we had woven. Just as I bent down to pick them up, the wake-up horns blared through the village and I instinctively clapped my hands to my ears until they finally subsided.
      
~
      
      The next few days dragged by without anything out of the ordinary occurring. I harvested those silk flowers for hours alongside my sisters while Cameren and Mother's duty was to work at the washing station and clean the plants. I met Talon again in the forest, and the vampire accompanied me to the next village over. What had once been the most stressful three miles of my life was suddenly not as frightening.
      Initially I had been wary of the vampire, but that fear quickly faded. I had been too quick to judge her. Throughout my life, I had always tried my best to be a friend to those who needed one the most. Talon was no exception-there were still kind people in the world.
      Now I was working in the fields, and as I tossed yet another flower into the basket, a horn suddenly blared from the village. I looked up in confusion. Dread was already seeping into the fabric of my being, and it took me a moment to realize that I was shivering. My hands were so cold that they felt as if they were on fire. It was the burning sensation before they numbed entirely. When they went numb, I beat them on my skirts until I regained feeling.
      "The overseers haven't called a meeting in years," Chloe-Jane whispered worriedly. "Do you think this might be about the shoes we are hoping for?"
      "I wouldn't bet on it," Clarity said under her breath.
      I just grimaced as I rose to my feet. I brushed the frozen mud and dirt from my skirts. My back ached and my fingers were covered in blisters.
      After we all crowded into the village square, a royal messenger stepped onto a crate and cleared his throat. "The king," he announced, shaking his head as if he couldn't believe what he was saying, "the king is dead."
      I looked back and forth between Chloe-Jane and Clarity, but my sisters had the same surprised expression painted on their faces. What did the death of the king mean for the enslaved? Confused murmurs flooded the square as the messenger's words finally registered.
      The messenger held up a hand and silence fell once again. "The king died five weeks ago, and the crown prince-the sole heir to the throne-has succeeded him."
      The prince? I knew better than to get my hopes up. While I didn't know anything about him, I doubted that the prince would be better than his father. The king had died five weeks ago? Of course we hadn't heard about it until now. Information always seemed to take its sweet time to reach us.
      "You are to keep working as if nothing has changed," the messenger continued. "The new king is coming to inspect the farm and then give out new orders at a future date."
      "When exactly is the king coming to inspect this farm?" one of the overseers called out.
      The messenger blinked as if the answer was obvious. "Tomorrow. Why else would I be here?"
      Tomorrow? Chloe-Jane mouthed as we looked at each other. I shook my head in bewilderment. The previous king had never visited our farm himself-whenever there was an inspection, the archduke was always sent in his place.
      "Is that all?" the overseer asked. The messenger nodded, and the overseer stepped up onto the crate. "Get back to work, all of you!"
      I silently followed my sisters out of the square. When we returned to the fields, Clarity let out an irritated sigh. "If the new king is anything like his father, the odds that we'll ever leave this wretched place just got even slimmer. Why couldn't the person who killed the king wipe out the rest of the royal family while they were at it?"
      "That's a little harsh," I said lightly, even though I was inclined to agree. "Come to think of it, though, I don't remember the messenger even telling us how the king died."
      "He obviously was assassinated." Clarity gave a nod in my direction as her breath puffed out in front of her. "There's no way the king's health could have failed-he was the king after all. No one could have been healthier than him."
      "You're right," Chloe-Jane piped up. "With all the best doctors and healers in the kingdom, it couldn't have been due to ill health."
      "But who," I pushed. "Who do you think killed him?"
      Clarity shrugged, losing interest. "There are plenty of people who had a grudge against him, plenty of people who wanted that power for themselves."
      Chloe-Jane suddenly looked up. "The prince. Didn't he just turn eighteen this year? Now that he's an adult, do you think he wanted to claim that power for himself?"
      "Maybe." Clarity wiped the frozen sweat from her brow. "Maybe not."
      "If that is the case," Chloe-Jane said anxiously, "our new king is evil. If he doesn't have any sympathy for his own father, how is he going to have any sympathy for his subjects?"
      Clarity tossed a handful of flowers into her basket. "That's not our problem. As long as we keep a low profile, he'll never look twice at us."
      I sighed as I looked up at the overcast sky. We didn't need another thing to worry about.
       
~
       
      The next day, I found myself constantly glancing up from the fields, expecting the king to appear any moment in the distance.
      "Corinna," Chloe-Jane said as she tugged on my sleeve. "Look."
      I followed my sister's gaze to the edge of the field. I could barely make out a group of knights on horseback.
      "That must be the king," Clarity said darkly as she gestured to the golden-haired figure at the front of the group.
      As they drew closer, I could see the gold crown glinting on the king's head.
      "Insufferable nobles," Clarity muttered under her breath.
      "The king's looking this way!" Chloe-Jane exclaimed worriedly before ducking down behind a cluster of frozen bushes.
      "Don't do that!" Clarity hissed in exasperation. "You're just making yourself look suspicious!"
      "If he can't see me, I can't be all that suspicious!" Chloe-Jane shot back. My younger sister shielded her head with her arms as if something would fall out of the sky.
      I continued to watch the king as he slowly surveyed the large stretch of farmland in front of him. Then the king turned and steered his horse back the way he had come. The knights turned as well and followed the lead of the young man with the golden hair.
      When the sun finally dropped from the sky, the sounds of the horns blared, instructing us to return to our houses. The next day was upon us before I could blink, and we were all summoned to the village square yet again.
      "The king has new orders for all of you," an overseer addressed us. "You are to double your daily amount of crops harvested."
      Double? My heart sank. We could barely make our daily total as it was.
      The overseer's cold eyes swept over the crowd. "For those of you who cannot meet the requirements, you will be punished." The overseer placed a hand on the leather whip at his side for emphasis.
      I felt Chloe-Jane grab my arm from next to me. "Is doubling our harvest even possible?" she whispered shrilly.
      "For us, maybe," I whispered back, trying my best to remain calm. "But the very young and the very elderly will struggle."
      "You are to weigh your baskets at the end of the day to make sure you have met the new requirement," the overseer called. "Now, get to work!"
      Clarity, Chloe, and I hurried back to the field and frantically dropped down to pick the string-like flowers. We were on our knees in the frozen mud for hours, and it didn't help in the slightest when the overseers walked up and down the rows of flowers to crack their whips at our bent backs. My back was sore and aching by the time the horns blared. We had met our requirements-barely.
      I wiped the frozen sweat from my face as I stood up. My vision swayed. At some point, my chapped lips had cracked and I could taste my own blood in my mouth. I staggered.
      Clarity grabbed my arm before I could topple backward onto the icy ground. "Careful." She met my gaze before turning to help Chloe-Jane to her feet.
      I began to unbraid my hair, but I paused as my frostbitten hands cried out in protest. I held out my hands, and in the silvery light from the moon, I could see the many cuts and pricks that peppered my skin. The flowers we were harvesting grew amid thorn bushes, and the fact that the flowering buds grew back within twenty-four hours made sure there was always endless work. Such a plant shouldn't be allowed to exist.
      "We should go," I said quietly, bending down to scoop up my basket. My sisters nodded and picked up their harvest before following me back toward the village square.
      The three of us stood in line with the other slaves, waiting for our turn to place our baskets on the scales. As we drew closer to the weighing station, there was a sudden clatter as a basket was knocked to the ground. I instinctively whirled toward the sound to find a woman a few years younger than my mother standing before one of the scales. An overseer was glaring down at her. "Didn't you hear what would happen if you didn't meet the new requirement, girl?" he demanded.
      "I'm sorry, I'm sorry." The woman took a trembling step back. "I-I was helping my youngest daughter finish hers, and I couldn't-"
      "No excuses," the overseer said icily as he unfurled the whip attached to his belt.
      I slid my free hand over Chloe-Jane's eyes and dropped my own gaze to the ground as the sound of leather against flesh cracked through the air. The woman's screams were followed shortly by the metallic smell of blood slowly drifting through the frozen air.
      The overseer finally lowered his bloody whip. "Take her away." Two other women hurried forward. They slung the first woman's arms over their shoulders and helped her stagger out of the village square.
      "It's over," I said shakingly as I removed my hand from Chloe-Jane's eyes. My own stomach had clenched painfully. I recognized that woman. She was a mother of three.
      The overseer turned to look coldly over at the rest of us standing in the line. "That could be any of you. Make sure not to disappoint if you don't want to be punished."
      Chloe-Jane trembled beside me, and I followed her gaze to the pool of blood staining the snow a deep crimson.
      "We're up next," Clarity told us softly.
      I walked up to the scale and placed my basket on top.
      "You pass," the overseer monitoring the scale told me listlessly. I nodded silently before taking my basket to the large barrel of flowers and emptied the contents into it. My sisters followed me and did the same. When we finished, we quietly shuffled out of the square as fast as we could and returned to our house.
      Mother and Cameren were already inside, and the former was tending to the fire in the hearth. Cameren was sitting with his back to the wall. He was applying the medicine I had retrieved for him on the large gashes that covered his arms and legs.
      "Here"-I hurried over to my brother after I shut the door behind us-"let me do that for you."
      "Are the three of you doing alright?" Cameren placed a hand on my arm as he looked up at me with worried eyes. The scars on his face were illuminated by the fiery light, and I couldn't help but remember the heartbreaking way my brother looked when he first returned from the war. "I was confident that you could manage to meet the new order, but it didn't stop me from worrying." Then Cameren's eyes fell on the cuts peppering my hands. "Corinna! Your hands-"
      "Don't you worry about us-all you need to do is take care of yourself and heal." I took the bottle of medicine from Cameren and dipped my fingers into the paste inside.
      Cameren gave me a grateful smile, but his eyes were still concerned.
      "Did you see what happened to the woman in the village square?" Clarity asked as she marched over to sit down on our straw bed.
      Mother sighed tiredly. "We didn't see it. But we heard her screams."
      Chloe-Jane shuddered and wrapped her arms around herself. "It's always horrible." Mother walked over to wrap her own arms around her daughter. Chloe-Jane buried her head in Mother's chest, still shaking.
      "Did you see the sky outside?" Mother asked softly as she stroked Chloe-Jane's hair.
      "The sky?" I had been so busy thinking about what had happened in the village square that I hadn't been paying attention to the night sky.
      It was Cameren's turn to sigh. "A storm is coming." 
      "A storm? When will it get here? How long will it last?"
      "Tomorrow," Cameren replied grimly as his dark eyes met mine.
      I stared blankly at my brother, half hoping that he was joking. But Cameren didn't make jokes anymore.
      I walked over to join Mother and Chloe-Jane at the hearth after I finished applying the medicine to my brother's wounds. "This could just be the first of many orders from the new king."
      "The temperatures keep dropping as well," Clarity added stoically. "At this rate, who knows if we'll make it to next year?"
      I gave my sister an exasperated look. "Don't say that."
      "You don't want to hear the truth?" Clarity shot back.
      "A little optimism won't hurt." Even though I wouldn't admit it, especially in front of Chloe-Jane, Clarity was right. If things continued the way they were now, there was no guarantee that all five of us would survive the winter. Clarity and I wouldn't go down without a fight, but Cameren was still recovering from life-threatening injuries, and Mother was not as young as she used to be. I fell silent as my heart sank even lower.
       
~
       
      When I opened the door the following morning, I was greeted with a deafening roar and a rush of icy wind. I shuddered, already chilled to the bone.
      "This can't be real," Chloe-Jane said weakly as she looked out at the raging storm. Snow was blowing in every direction and the sun was completely hidden by the sea of dark gray clouds. After wolfing down our breakfast in the village square, Chloe-Jane, Clarity, and I sprinted as fast as we could toward the field.
      Our day was a grueling one, and it took all of my willpower and strength to force myself to keep going. When the sun finally set and the horns blared in the distance, I shakily climbed to my feet. Everything hurt. A strong blast of wind swept over the field and I had to snatch up my basket to keep it from being carried away.
      "Be careful," I warned my sisters as they picked up their baskets as well. "Move slowly and steadily. We have ten minutes to get back." Another gust of wind swept over the field and I threw my free arm in front of my face to prevent the icy wind from tearing at my eyes.
      Chloe-Jane let out a cry from behind me, and I whirled around to find that my sister had fallen face-first into the snow.
      "Chloe!" I dropped to my knees beside her. "Are you alright?"
      "I slipped." Chloe-Jane stifled a sob as she sat up. Her lips were cracked, and she brushed snow from her hair and face. "I can't feel my feet. And m-my basket," she said through chattering teeth, "I-I dropped it!"
      I quickly stood up and frantically scanned the field for the large basket.
      "There!" Clarity suddenly pointed.
      "Hold this!" I pushed my basket into Chloe-Jane's arms before turning to dash through the storm.
      "Corinna!" Chloe-Jane called after me, but her voice was swept away by the winds.
      My frozen limbs screamed in protest as I darted through the rows of thorny bushes. All I knew was that if I didn't retrieve the basket, Chloe-Jane would be in big trouble.
      In one swift move, I scooped up Chloe-Jane's basket before it could tumble further away. I flipped open the basket's lid and my heart nearly stopped. At least half of Chloe-Jane's flowers had scattered in the wind. Gritting my teeth, I turned and hurried back toward my sisters. "I got it," I told Chloe-Jane as I returned to the two of them. "Now let's hurry!"
      "Wait!" Chloe-Jane panicked. "Shouldn't we swap baskets? I still have yours!"
      "Later," I panted. "I don't know how much time we have left. If we're late, we'll all get punished."
      Fortunately, by the time we made it back to the village square, we weren't the last ones back. We were able to join the line of slaves waiting to weigh their baskets, and judging by the faint scent of frozen blood in the air, it looked like someone hadn't been able to meet the requirement today. I just hoped that whoever they were, they were okay. Then I let out a humorless laugh.
      "What are you laughing at?" Clarity asked sharply. Her eyes were narrow as she scrutinized me.
      I shook my head. My heart rate had increased to the point where I was finding breathing difficult. "It's nothing."
      When we reached the scales, I waited until Chloe-Jane and Clarity had finished weighing their baskets before stepping forward. At this point, I couldn't tell if I was shaking from the cold or the fear of what was about to come.
      "Girl." The overseer slowly looked down at me. "You saw what happened yesterday, and yet you still bring me a half-empty basket?"
      I tried my best not to flinch as the overseer threw the basket to the ground at my feet. Pale flowers spilled into the snow before being swept up by the wind. I kept my eyes on the ground as I heard the overseer crack his whip.
      "Turn around, girl," the overseer said coldly.
      Drawing in one uneven breath after the other, I slowly turned on the spot. Before I had fully finished pivoting, the overseer's whip bit into my back and I lost my footing. I tumbled to the snow-covered ground with a cry. My back was on fire as my vision went red, and my frozen hands felt like they were tearing apart as I curled my fingers into fists.
      I refused to look back at my sisters as the whip struck me again and again and again. The last thing I could remember before I blacked out was the excruciating pain, my own screaming, and then Chloe-Jane and Clarity half-carrying, half-dragging me back to our house as they called my name.
       
~
       
      "Corinna, Corinna, Corinna." I awoke to the sound of Mother softly calling my name.
      I blinked and squinted against the blinding light. I was lying on my stomach, and my back burned as if someone had laid hot irons on top of it. I hissed as white spots flared across my vision.
      "Corinna!" I heard Chloe-Jane's sob. "Why did you do that? It was my basket-why did you do that?"
      I just winced. Her voice shot a new series of needles through my skull.
      "Chloe," Cameren called in a hushed voice, "a little quieter, okay?"
      "Don't move, Corinna," Clarity's voice commanded from somewhere behind me. "I cleaned and bandaged your wounds the best I could."
      "Clarity, where did you manage to find bandages?" My voice was raspy.
      "Don't ask," Clarity said shortly.
      I fell silent. "How long was I out?" I asked instead.
      "Two days," Mother informed me.
      My head spun. "Two days?"
      "The overseers say you're supposed to be back in the fields by tomorrow," Chloe-Jane said worriedly. This time, her voice was considerably softer.
      I squeezed my eyes shut and took one shallow breath after the other. I could barely move without my back screaming in protest. Could I work in my current condition? I didn't have much of a choice.
      "How are you feeling?" Chloe-Jane asked.
      I closed my eyes. "I'm still alive."
      Even though I was drifting in and out of consciousness, I could feel the constant warmth of a hand around mine. It was Mother. "Try to get some more sleep," she told me softly. "Tomorrow is going to be another long day."
      I could only nod and close my eyes. All my energy was completely spent, and before I knew it, I had drifted off into a sea of memories.
      
~
      
      I heard Clarity's voice. "You're doing it all wrong." My shoulders curved inward as I turned to face her, and I wiped the sweat from my brow with the back of my hand. I remembered this day: it was the middle of summer, ten years ago, and the sun spared us no mercy as it beat down over us. "If you pick them from the stems that way-"
      "I can't anymore, Clarity," I rasped. My throat was parched and my head muddied. "I don't feel very good."
      Cameren's figure swam into my vision next to Clarity. "This is her first summer working on the fields, her first month too-everything will come into place with a little practice."
      "Cameren," I said as I looked up at my brother. My hands were just as raw as my throat.
      Cameren crouched down to gently tuck a loose curl behind my ear. "Don't pick anymore. Look! Mother's on her way over right now-she's bringing all of us a drink of water."
      "Corinna!" Mother dropped down at my side. "Quick-have some water."
      I took the bowl from her and gulped down the liquid. The world around me seemed to clear up, and I squinted against the brilliant sunlight. Then I dropped my gaze to my feet. "I'm sorry-it's almost time for the midday inspection and I haven't finished my basket."
      The words had only just left my mouth when an overseer approached us. "Whose basket is this?" his voice demanded. I felt my body tense up, and fear flooded through every fiber of my being.
      "I-" My voice was no more than a whisper as I trembled.
      "It's mine." My head snapped up as I watched Mother step in front of me, shielding me from the overseer.
      "Being lazy, are we now, girl?" the overseer asked nastily. "Trying to test your luck, eh? Just because you used to serve the master himself doesn't give you special privileges . . ."
      The sound of a whip cracking shattered the air, and my screams bled together with Clarity's and Cameren's. "MOTHER!"
      I couldn't stop tears from streaming down my face even after we had returned to our house. Mother lay on the pile of husks we used as a bed with her brow furrowed in pain.
      I sat there next to her and held her hand tightly in mine. I wouldn't let go.
      "Corinna," Father called my name as he sat down next to me.
      I finally tore my gaze away from Mother to look up at him.
      "It's late," Father sighed softly. "We've got another long day ahead of us tomorrow-you should try to get some sleep. You've done a wonderful job looking after your mother, but I can take it from here."
      I blinked. I hadn't registered the passage of time. Looking around, I realized that Chloe-Jane had returned from the class all the children under the age of seven were required to attend. I had been there with her until just a month ago. I shook my head. "I don't think I'll be able to sleep without Mother braiding my hair the way she does every night."
      "How about you let me try?" Father suggested. There was a faint smile on his face. "I chop wood all day-braiding hair should be an easy task."
      "Me too, me too!" Chloe-Jane sat on the other side of Father as she tugged on his sleeve expectantly. "Braid my hair too!"
      Father laughed with warm eyes. "One at a time."
      "I can do Chloe's," Cameren offered.
      "I doubt you know how," Clarity said shortly with a glance in Cameren's direction. "Here, let me teach you."
      I saw Mother flash Father a grateful smile. I looked up at Father, but suddenly, the pain in my back returned, and the memory faded before I could see his face one last time. As I drifted in and out of consciousness, my eyes stung with silent tears, and my hand tightened around Mother's.
       
~
       
      The next week painfully dragged past, and by the end of it, we were all exhausted to the point of collapsing into the snow. I hadn't traveled to the forest either because of my condition, and I hoped the medicine girl didn't think that I was dead. We still had enough medicine for Cameren for now, but if I didn't get better before it ran out, Clarity would go in my stead.
      When the horns blared in the morning, I slowly sat up. My back was still sore and tender, but there was nothing more I could do about it. Before I could leave the house, another horn sounded. A village meeting was being called. We filed into the village square and looked up wearily at the overseers standing at the front of the group.
      "There has been a new order from the king," one of the overseers said loudly as he addressed the crowd. "All girls between the ages of eight and fifteen are to be taken to the capital city. Young seamstresses are in high demand."
      I looked down in horror to see that Chloe-Jane had frozen. Her eyes and mouth were wide open in shock. This had to be some kind of misunderstanding. They couldn't take Chloe-Jane away!
      "If you are a girl between eight and fifteen years old," the overseer called as he eyed the crowd, "step forward immediately to get documented. You'll wait at the edge of the village. The carts that will collect you will arrive at noon." When no one moved, the overseer scowled in annoyance. "We'll do this the hard way, then." He nodded to the other overseers surrounding the village square. Despaired cries filled the air as the overseers forcefully made their way through the crowd of slaves and dragged out all of the young girls as they went.
      Chloe-Jane frantically grabbed my sleeve as the overseers drew closer. "They're going to take me away, Corinna!"
      I held my sister's gaze as my panic began to sink in. "I'll try to think of something. I won't let them take you."
      Suddenly Chloe-Jane was forcefully yanked from my grasp as an overseer tugged her into the crowd.
      "Chloe!" I retrieved her hand and pulled her back toward me. Somewhere behind me, Clarity was yelling Chloe-Jane's name, but her voice was lost in the clamor around us. Mother and Cameren were nowhere to be seen and I could feel my panic taking over.
      "Why, you-" the overseer seethed as he lifted his hand. I knew what was coming before the blow to my face sent me staggering backward.
      "Corinna!" Chloe-Jane sobbed as she was dragged away. She thrashed and kicked, but her struggles were in vain.
      All I could do was look on in horror as Chloe-Jane and the overseer vanished from sight.
      Noon. I had until noon to rescue my sister. I had been doing everything I could to keep the remaining members of my family together. After Father had been drafted all those years ago, an unfillable void was left behind. At the time, we were all so young that Chloe-Jane didn't even have a clear memory of our kind, loving father. 
      The overseers couldn't take Chloe-Jane away. If she was taken to the capital city, we would never be able to see her again. And how would fourteen-year-old Chloe-Jane survive in the capital city all alone?
      "Get back to work," the overseer called down to the rest of us once all the young girls had been removed from the crowd. "You're still expected to meet the requirements for today's harvest."
      "Corinna." Clarity tugged at my sleeve. "We need to go."
      I whirled to face my older sister. "Our sister just got dragged away!"
      Clarity glared at me with steely eyes. "If we try to do anything, we'll just be causing her more trouble!"
      "Don't you want her to stay with us?" I asked desperately. "Don't you want our family to stay together?"
      "Of course I do! But there's only so much we can do without putting ourselves in danger as well."
      "I'm going to save her," I vowed.
      "You can't," Clarity said as she dragged me toward the field. "That won't be possible without you getting yourself seriously injured or even killed. And what would I do if anything happened to you?"
      I shook myself free from Clarity's arms but continued following her toward the field. "Help me, then. We'll get her away from the overseers together."
      Clarity gave me a sideways look. "Let's say we do get her away from the overseers-then what?"
      I held my sister's gaze. "I'm going to get all of us out of here one day."
      Clarity turned her back to me. "Do as you will. But you're in this alone. If anything goes wrong, I want you to remember that it's on you, not me."
      When we reached the field, I set my basket down on the dirt. Instead of crouching down to pick the flowers, I straightened and stood upright. Standing there in the middle of the field, I looked around at the land spreading out around me. This land had been all I ever knew. My body ached as I gazed out at the horizon. My life was anything but ideal. But while I didn't have freedom, I did have a family. I had my siblings. We had grown up together, and those brief moments of joy we shared were something I held dearly within me. And so as those memories played in my head and heart, I turned on my heel and began to carefully make my way back toward the village.
      I crept through the now silent village, careful to avoid the watchful eyes of overseers on the way. Judging from the direction the girls had been dragged, I concluded that Chloe-Jane and the others were being kept in the overseers' meeting hall.
      When I finally arrived at the hall, I silently tiptoed up to a window. As I had predicted, all of the young girls were huddled inside. Two overseers stood in front of the door, deep in conversation. I scanned the sea of faces for Chloe-Jane and quickly spotted her among the crowd.
      After taking a deep breath, I quietly pushed the window open. Fortunately I didn't need to distract the guards. They were so busy talking that they barely moved at all as I silently slipped into the room. If any of the girls noticed me, they didn't react.
      Keeping my head lowered, I moved toward my sister as quickly as I dared. "Chloe," I whispered.
      Chloe-Jane's head snapped up to look at me. Her eyes were wide with fright. "What are you doing here? Do you know what will happen if they catch you?"
      I gently took my sister's warm hand and laced my frozen fingers with hers. "I'm getting you out of here. Follow me."
      Chloe-Jane nodded and I slowly led her back toward the window. When we reached it, I quickly climbed outside before turning to help my sister over the ledge.
      "Stop!" a gruff voice commanded from behind me before I could lift Chloe-Jane up. I froze. "Don't move, girl."
      I held my breath as I heard the sound of a blade being unsheathed. I had only heard it a handful of times before, and it sent fear rippling through my core.
      "Run!" Chloe-Jane screamed as she shoved me with all the strength she could muster. "Run!"
      After painfully tearing my gaze from my sister, I turned and sprinted toward the forest. I swerved past the first overseer, then the next one, and the next. Why are there suddenly so many overseers surrounding the building? I thought in a panic. But my question was answered a split second later as my eyes fell on the carts parked a short distance off: it was already noon.
      "Stop running!" an overseer shouted angrily at me.
      I willed myself to run even faster. My heart pounded in my chest and my breath felt like glass in my throat. As I entered the forest, I could hear the footsteps of my many pursuers, but I refused to look back.
      "Talon!" I shouted desperately. Then another thought struck me: could the vampire even hear me from this distance? My heart sank. I couldn't run forever, and my back was beginning to strain.
      Suddenly, there was a startled yell from behind me, followed by the sound of bodies thudding to the ground. I skidded to a stop when I realized that the footsteps had fallen silent. I slowly turned to look over my shoulder.
      Five overseers lay unmoving on the forest floor and I could sense the presence of someone standing next to me.

Chapter Four
       
"Talon!" Corinna cried as she spun around to face me. I could see relief fill her eyes as she slumped down against a tree and clutched her heart with a raw, frozen hand.
      "What happened?" I asked worriedly.
      "Long story short, I tried and failed to rescue my sister from being taken away to the capital city."
      I blinked. "The capital city?"
      Corinna nodded in defeat. "The new king wants all the girls between eight and fifteen years old to be taken to the capital. It was something about having a shortage of seamstresses . . . or so they claimed."
      "When are they taking them?"
      "Now."
      "Follow me." I quickly made my way toward the edge of the forest. Given that the sun was still up, I made sure to remain in the shadow of the trees when the village came into view. "Where's your sister?" I turned to Corinna, but the young woman was nowhere to be found. "Corinna?"
      Retracing my steps, I found Corinna staggering through the trees toward me. "Not everyone can move as fast as you, Talon," Corinna said, still out of breath.
      "I'm sorry," I apologized hastily. This time we made our way to the forest's edge together. "Where's your sister?" I asked again.
      Corinna fell silent as she peered through the bushes. There were fresh cart tracks in the snow leading toward the main road. "She's gone. I-I don't believe it. All of the girls are gone." Corinna dropped to her knees and turned to me with a look of helplessness in her eyes. "Please, I'm begging you-I can't let them take my sister like that!"
      I held Corinna's gaze as I knelt down and lifted her back onto her feet. "I'm sure you know that if a vampire is exposed to direct sunlight, they lose their strength and speed and skin. Besides, with so many carts and escorts, I wouldn't overestimate my own abilities. It's true that I could run in there, but I don't know what your sister looks like. Even if I did, I might be able to get her out, but I'm afraid of what it would mean for the safety of the other girls."
      "What do I do?" Corinna asked hopelessly. She paced back and forth as she tugged at her hair.
      "What do you want to do?"
      Corinna took a deep breath. "First I need to get my family out of the village. After that I'll make my way to the capital city." Then she looked up at me again and determination sparked in her eyes. "Will you help me?"
       
~
       
      Corinna sat in the clearing, huddled in the cloak and boots I had brought out for her. The forest, with its tall trees and mist and shadows, was already cold for a human-and it would be even more so by nightfall. "Do you know how many more hours until sunset?" she asked quietly.
      "About two more now," I answered softly. "I'll start getting ready."
      After leaving the clearing, I made my way to the hollowed-out log where I stashed clothes and weapons. I pulled out the different parts of the overseer's uniforms from inside it: tunic, trousers, boots, armored chest plate, and helmet. With this outfit, I would be able to go in and out of the village without arousing any suspicions. The iron chest plate completely hid my breasts and the helmet nearly covered my entire face. Once I finished changing into the uniform, I made my way back to Corinna.
      Corinna jumped to her feet when I emerged from the trees. "Talon-you really do look like an overseer!"
      I removed the helmet from my head and set it down on the ground. Then I brushed my long hair over my shoulder and twisted it between my delicate fingers. In one swift movement, I sliced it all off with a razor-sharp nail.
      "What are you doing-?" Corinna stared in shock at the inky strands floating to the ground around my feet.
      "Cutting it off. I'd be too obvious otherwise. It will grow back within a couple of days. Are you ready?"
      Corinna nodded her head.
      I tucked my helmet under my arm. "In that case, we'll move to the edge of the forest to wait."
      When the sun faded from the sky, the peak of the rising moon was my cue to make my way into the village. The overseers I passed did nothing more than nod in my direction as I walked by. According to Corinna, there was only one overseer who watched the section of the village where her house was located. I made my way toward the location based on the map Corinna had previously drawn out in the dirt. When I found the overseer, I quickly knocked him unconscious before walking back to the forest to alert Corinna that it was safe to come out.
      Corinna hurried into the village and made a beeline for her house. I waited outside the small building as Corinna silently slipped inside. "Mother," I heard Corinna's whisper, "wake up."
      "Corinna?" a woman's voice said in surprise. "I was so worried-when you didn't come back, I . . ."
      "You're back." Another woman spoke up. Her voice was as sharp as a knife.
      "Yes," Corinna said, "I am. And I'm getting all of you out of here."
      "Out of here?" a man's voice asked. "What do you mean?"
      "Please." Corinna's voice was full of emotion. "Just trust me."
      There was a soft creaking as the door opened. Corinna was the first to cross the threshold and was closely followed by three others. The older woman, who must have been Corinna's mother, was the first to notice me. Her eyes were fearful as she squinted at me through the dark. The other woman, Corinna's sister, saw me shortly after. She stared at me defiantly with her head held high and eyes full of steel.
      "It's okay," Corinna quickly reassured them as she placed a gentle hand on her mother's arm. "We just need to move quickly."
      I let Corinna take the lead, and she motioned for her family to follow her toward the forest. As we hurried toward the trees, I heard Corinna's brother ask her under his breath, "Did you really manage to convince an overseer to help you? Can he be trusted?"
      "She," Corinna corrected. "She's not actually an overseer. And as a matter of fact, Cameren, I do trust her."
      Once reached the trees, I led the group to the clearing in the middle of the forest. "We'll stay here until morning. Then we can start making our way toward the capital."
      "The capital?" Hope bloomed in Corinna's eyes as she turned to look at me. "You're going to take us to the capital?"
      I inclined my head. It would be nice to have company for a change-also I would be able to ask around about Karmen as we traveled.
      Corinna smiled tiredly. "Thank you."
      "Wait." Corinna's sister placed a hand on her hip as she gave me a piercing, dark look. "What are we doing in the middle of the forest? What about all the monsters that are lurking in this place? Corinna can't speak for all of us. I don't trust you."
      Corinna tugged at her sister's sleeve. "Clarity-"
      Clarity just brushed her sister off. "Whoever you are, whatever you are," Clarity continued, "if you hurt my sister in any way, you will regret it."
